### Section: Writing Algebraic Expressions

In the previous section, we learned about substitution and evaluating expressions, which involved finding the value of an algebraic expression when specific values are substituted for the variables. Now, let's focus on the first step in this process: writing algebraic expressions.

An algebraic expression is a mathematical phrase that contains variables, numbers, and operations. It represents a relationship or a rule that can be used to solve problems and make calculations. Writing algebraic expressions is an essential skill in algebra, as it allows us to translate real-world situations into mathematical language.

#### Subsection: Translating Words into Algebra

Translating words into algebraic expressions involves converting verbal descriptions or word problems into mathematical expressions. This process helps us represent the given information in a concise and precise manner, making it easier to solve problems.

To translate words into algebra, follow these steps:

1. Identify the unknown quantity: Read the problem carefully and determine what the problem is asking for. This will be the variable or the unknown quantity in your algebraic expression.

2. Define the variable: Assign a letter or symbol to represent the unknown quantity. Choose a letter that is relevant to the problem and easy to work with.

3. Determine the operations: Look for keywords or phrases that indicate mathematical operations. Common keywords include "sum," "difference," "product," and "quotient." Use these keywords to determine the appropriate operations to include in your expression.

4. Write the expression: Combine the variable, numbers, and operations to create the algebraic expression. Make sure to use parentheses when necessary to indicate the order of operations.

Let's look at an example to understand the process of translating words into algebraic expressions:

Example 1:
A store sells t-shirts for $15 each. Let's write an algebraic expression to represent the total cost of buying x t-shirts.

Solution:
1. Identify the unknown quantity: The problem is asking for the total cost of buying t-shirts, which is the unknown quantity.

2. Define the variable: Let's use the variable "C" to represent the total cost.

3. Determine the operations: The problem states that each t-shirt costs $15, so we need to multiply the number of t-shirts by $15.

4. Write the expression: The algebraic expression for the total cost of buying x t-shirts is $15 multiplied by x, which can be written as $15x.

Now that we have learned how to write algebraic expressions, we can move on to the next step of evaluating these expressions by substituting values into them.

### Section: Writing Algebraic Expressions

In the previous section, we learned about substitution and evaluating expressions, which involved finding the value of an algebraic expression when specific values are substituted for the variables. Now, let's focus on the first step in this process: writing algebraic expressions.

An algebraic expression is a mathematical phrase that contains variables, numbers, and operations. It represents a relationship or a rule that can be used to solve problems and make calculations. Writing algebraic expressions is an essential skill in algebra, as it allows us to translate real-world situations into mathematical language.

#### Subsection: Translating Words into Algebra

Translating words into algebraic expressions involves converting verbal descriptions or word problems into mathematical expressions. This process helps us represent the given information in a concise and precise manner, making it easier to solve problems.

To translate words into algebra, follow these steps:

1. Identify the unknown quantity: Read the problem carefully and determine what the problem is asking for. This will be the variable or the unknown quantity in your algebraic expression.

2. Define the variable: Assign a letter or symbol to represent the unknown quantity. Choose a letter that is relevant to the problem and easy to work with.

3. Determine the operations: Look for keywords or phrases that indicate mathematical operations. Common keywords include "sum," "difference," "product," and "quotient." Use these keywords to determine the appropriate operations to include in your expression.

4. Write the expression: Combine the variable, numbers, and operations to create the algebraic expression. Make sure to use parentheses when necessary to indicate the order of operations.

Let's look at an example to understand the process of translating words into algebraic expressions:

Example 1:
A store sells t-shirts for $15 each. Let's write an algebraic expression to represent the total cost of buying "x" t-shirts.

Solution:
1. Identify the unknown quantity: The unknown quantity in this problem is the total cost of buying t-shirts.

2. Define the variable: Let's use the variable "C" to represent the total cost.

3. Determine the operations: The problem states that each t-shirt costs $15, so we need to multiply the number of t-shirts by $15 to find the total cost.

4. Write the expression: The algebraic expression to represent the total cost of buying "x" t-shirts is:

$$C = 15x$$

In this expression, "C" represents the total cost, and "x" represents the number of t-shirts.

Now that we have learned how to write algebraic expressions, let's practice with some problems.

#### Subsection: Practice Problems

1. Write an algebraic expression to represent the area of a rectangle with length "L" and width "W".

2. The cost of a concert ticket is $50. Write an algebraic expression to represent the total cost of buying "n" tickets.

3. The temperature in degrees Celsius can be converted to degrees Fahrenheit using the formula: $$F = \frac{9}{5}C + 32$$. Write an algebraic expression to represent the temperature in degrees Fahrenheit given the temperature in degrees Celsius.

Take your time to solve these problems, and refer back to the steps we discussed earlier if you need help.

### Section: Writing Algebraic Expressions

In the previous section, we learned about substitution and evaluating expressions, which involved finding the value of an algebraic expression when specific values are substituted for the variables. Now, let's focus on the first step in this process: writing algebraic expressions.

An algebraic expression is a mathematical phrase that contains variables, numbers, and operations. It represents a relationship or a rule that can be used to solve problems and make calculations. Writing algebraic expressions is an essential skill in algebra, as it allows us to translate real-world situations into mathematical language.

#### Subsection: Real World Applications

Algebraic expressions are not just abstract concepts; they have real-world applications that can help us solve everyday problems. By translating real-world situations into algebraic expressions, we can use the power of algebra to analyze and solve these problems.

Let's explore some common real-world applications of algebraic expressions:

1. **Finance**: Algebraic expressions can be used to calculate interest, investments, and loans. For example, if you want to calculate the total amount of money in a savings account after a certain number of years, you can use the formula:

   $$A = P(1 + r)^t$$

   Where:
   - A represents the final amount of money in the account
   - P represents the initial principal (or starting amount)
   - r represents the interest rate (expressed as a decimal)
   - t represents the number of years

   By substituting the appropriate values for P, r, and t, you can calculate the final amount of money in the account.

2. **Geometry**: Algebraic expressions can be used to solve geometric problems. For example, if you want to find the area of a rectangle, you can use the formula:

   $$A = l \times w$$

   Where:
   - A represents the area of the rectangle
   - l represents the length of the rectangle
   - w represents the width of the rectangle

   By substituting the appropriate values for l and w, you can calculate the area of the rectangle.

3. **Physics**: Algebraic expressions are used extensively in physics to describe various physical phenomena. For example, if you want to calculate the distance traveled by an object with a constant velocity, you can use the formula:

   $$d = v \times t$$

   Where:
   - d represents the distance traveled
   - v represents the velocity of the object
   - t represents the time taken

   By substituting the appropriate values for v and t, you can calculate the distance traveled by the object.

These are just a few examples of how algebraic expressions can be applied to real-world situations. By mastering the skill of writing algebraic expressions, you will be able to solve a wide range of problems in various fields.

Now that we understand the importance of algebraic expressions in real-world applications, let's move on to the next section, where we will learn how to translate words into algebraic expressions.

### Section: Combining Like Terms

In the previous section, we learned about writing algebraic expressions, which involved translating real-world situations into mathematical language. Now, let's move on to the next step in algebraic manipulation: combining like terms.

#### Subsection: Definition and Rules

When simplifying algebraic expressions, it is often necessary to combine like terms. Like terms are terms that have the same variables raised to the same powers. By combining these terms, we can simplify the expression and make it easier to work with.

To combine like terms, we follow a set of rules:

1. **Identify like terms**: Look for terms that have the same variables raised to the same powers. For example, in the expression $3x + 2y + 5x - 4y$, the terms $3x$ and $5x$ are like terms because they both have the variable $x$ raised to the power of $1$, and the terms $2y$ and $-4y$ are like terms because they both have the variable $y$ raised to the power of $1$.

2. **Combine coefficients**: Add or subtract the coefficients of the like terms. In the example above, we can combine the coefficients of $3x$ and $5x$ to get $8x$, and the coefficients of $2y$ and $-4y$ to get $-2y$.

3. **Keep the variable**: Keep the variable and its exponent unchanged. In our example, we keep the variable $x$ raised to the power of $1$ and the variable $y$ raised to the power of $1$.

4. **Write the simplified expression**: Write the simplified expression by combining the like terms. In our example, the simplified expression is $8x - 2y$.

Combining like terms is an important skill in algebra, as it allows us to simplify expressions and solve equations more efficiently. By following the rules above, we can simplify complex expressions and make them easier to work with.

Now that we understand the concept of combining like terms, let's practice applying these rules to various examples in the upcoming exercises.

### Section: Combining Like Terms

In the previous section, we learned about writing algebraic expressions, which involved translating real-world situations into mathematical language. Now, let's move on to the next step in algebraic manipulation: combining like terms.

#### Subsection: Practice Problems

To reinforce our understanding of combining like terms, let's solve some practice problems. Remember, the key to combining like terms is to identify terms that have the same variables raised to the same powers and then combine their coefficients.

**Practice Problem 1:**

Simplify the expression $2x + 3y - 4x + 5y$.

**Solution:**

To combine like terms, we first identify the terms that have the same variables raised to the same powers. In this case, we have $2x$ and $-4x$ as like terms because they both have the variable $x$ raised to the power of $1$. Similarly, we have $3y$ and $5y$ as like terms because they both have the variable $y$ raised to the power of $1$.

Next, we combine the coefficients of the like terms. For $2x$ and $-4x$, we have $2 - 4 = -2$. For $3y$ and $5y$, we have $3 + 5 = 8$.

Finally, we write the simplified expression by combining the like terms. The simplified expression is $-2x + 8y$.

**Practice Problem 2:**

Simplify the expression $4a - 2b + 3a + 5b$.

**Solution:**

To combine like terms, we first identify the terms that have the same variables raised to the same powers. In this case, we have $4a$ and $3a$ as like terms because they both have the variable $a$ raised to the power of $1$. Similarly, we have $-2b$ and $5b$ as like terms because they both have the variable $b$ raised to the power of $1$.

Next, we combine the coefficients of the like terms. For $4a$ and $3a$, we have $4 + 3 = 7$. For $-2b$ and $5b$, we have $-2 + 5 = 3$.

Finally, we write the simplified expression by combining the like terms. The simplified expression is $7a + 3b$.

Now that we have practiced combining like terms, we are ready to move on to more advanced algebraic manipulations. Remember, combining like terms is an important skill in algebra, as it allows us to simplify expressions and solve equations more efficiently. By following the rules we learned, we can simplify complex expressions and make them easier to work with.

### Section: Understanding the Distributive Property

The distributive property is an important concept in algebra that allows us to simplify expressions and perform operations more efficiently. It is a fundamental rule that helps us manipulate algebraic expressions by distributing a value or term to each term inside parentheses.

#### Definition of the Distributive Property

The distributive property states that for any real numbers $a$, $b$, and $c$:

$$a \cdot (b + c) = a \cdot b + a \cdot c$$

In other words, when we have a number or term outside parentheses being multiplied by a sum of terms inside parentheses, we can distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses.

#### Rules for Using the Distributive Property

To effectively use the distributive property, we need to keep a few rules in mind:

1. **Distributing a number to a sum of terms:** When we have a number or term outside parentheses being multiplied by a sum of terms inside parentheses, we distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses. For example:

   $$2 \cdot (x + y) = 2 \cdot x + 2 \cdot y$$

2. **Distributing a number to a difference of terms:** Similar to distributing to a sum of terms, we can also distribute a number or term to a difference of terms inside parentheses. For example:

   $$3 \cdot (x - y) = 3 \cdot x - 3 \cdot y$$

3. **Distributing a term to a product of terms:** We can also distribute a term to a product of terms inside parentheses. For example:

   $$a \cdot (b \cdot c) = (a \cdot b) \cdot (a \cdot c)$$

   This rule is particularly useful when we have multiple terms inside parentheses that need to be multiplied by a common term.

4. **Distributing a negative sign:** When we have a negative sign outside parentheses, we can distribute it to each term inside the parentheses. For example:

   $$-2 \cdot (x + y) = -2 \cdot x - 2 \cdot y$$

   This rule is important to remember when dealing with negative coefficients or negative numbers outside parentheses.

By understanding and applying the distributive property, we can simplify algebraic expressions and make them easier to work with. Let's practice some problems to reinforce our understanding of this concept.

### Section: Understanding the Distributive Property

The distributive property is an important concept in algebra that allows us to simplify expressions and perform operations more efficiently. It is a fundamental rule that helps us manipulate algebraic expressions by distributing a value or term to each term inside parentheses.

1. **Distributing a number to a sum of terms:** When we have a number or term outside parentheses being multiplied by a sum of terms inside parentheses, we distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses. For example:

   $$2 \cdot (x + y) = 2 \cdot x + 2 \cdot y$$

### Section: Understanding the Distributive Property

The distributive property is an important concept in algebra that allows us to simplify expressions and perform operations more efficiently. It is a fundamental rule that helps us manipulate algebraic expressions by distributing a value or term to each term inside parentheses.

#### Definition of the Distributive Property

The distributive property states that for any real numbers $a$, $b$, and $c$:

$$a \cdot (b + c) = a \cdot b + a \cdot c$$

In other words, when we have a number or term outside parentheses being multiplied by a sum of terms inside parentheses, we can distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses.

#### Rules for Using the Distributive Property

To effectively use the distributive property, we need to keep a few rules in mind:

1. **Distributing a number to a sum of terms:** When we have a number or term outside parentheses being multiplied by a sum of terms inside parentheses, we distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses. For example:

   $$2 \cdot (x + y) = 2 \cdot x + 2 \cdot y$$

   This rule allows us to simplify expressions by multiplying each term inside the parentheses by the number or term outside the parentheses.

2. **Distributing a number to a difference of terms:** Similar to distributing to a sum of terms, we can also distribute a number or term to a difference of terms inside parentheses. For example:

   $$3 \cdot (x - y) = 3 \cdot x - 3 \cdot y$$

   This rule allows us to distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses, whether it is a positive or negative term.

3. **Distributing a term to a product of terms:** We can also distribute a term to a product of terms inside parentheses. For example:

   $$a \cdot (b \cdot c) = (a \cdot b) \cdot (a \cdot c)$$

   This rule is particularly useful when we have multiple terms inside parentheses that need to be multiplied by a common term. It allows us to simplify the expression by multiplying each term inside the parentheses by the term outside the parentheses.

4. **Distributing a negative sign:** When we have a negative sign outside parentheses, we can distribute it to each term inside the parentheses. For example:

   $$-2 \cdot (x + y) = -2 \cdot x - 2 \cdot y$$

   This rule is important to remember when dealing with negative coefficients or negative numbers outside parentheses. It allows us to distribute the negative sign to each term inside the parentheses.

### Subsection: Real World Applications

The distributive property may seem like an abstract concept, but it has real-world applications that can help us solve practical problems. By understanding and applying the distributive property, we can simplify expressions and perform calculations more efficiently.

One common application of the distributive property is in calculating the cost of multiple items. Let's say you want to buy 3 apples that cost $2 each and 4 oranges that cost $1.50 each. Instead of calculating the total cost separately for each item, we can use the distributive property to simplify the calculation:

$$3 \cdot (2) + 4 \cdot (1.50)$$

Using the distributive property, we can distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses:

$$6 + 6$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$12$$

So, the total cost of buying 3 apples and 4 oranges would be $12.

Another real-world application of the distributive property is in expanding and simplifying expressions in physics. For example, when calculating the work done by a force on an object, we often encounter expressions involving the product of force and displacement. By applying the distributive property, we can simplify these expressions and make calculations easier.

In summary, the distributive property is a powerful tool in algebra that allows us to simplify expressions and perform operations more efficiently. By understanding and applying the distributive property, we can solve real-world problems and make mathematical calculations easier.

### Section: Equivalent Algebraic Expressions

In algebra, we often encounter different expressions that represent the same mathematical relationship. These expressions are called equivalent algebraic expressions. Understanding equivalent expressions is crucial for simplifying and solving algebraic equations.

#### Definition of Equivalent Algebraic Expressions

Two algebraic expressions are considered equivalent if they have the same value for all possible values of the variables involved. In other words, no matter what values we substitute for the variables, the two expressions will always yield the same result.

For example, consider the expressions $2x + 3$ and $x + x + 3$. These expressions are equivalent because they represent the same relationship between the variable $x$ and the constant term $3$. No matter what value we assign to $x$, both expressions will give us the same result.

#### Properties of Equivalent Algebraic Expressions

Equivalent algebraic expressions possess certain properties that allow us to manipulate and simplify them. These properties are based on the fundamental rules of algebra and can be used to transform one expression into another while maintaining equivalence.

1. **Commutative Property of Addition and Multiplication:** The commutative property states that the order of terms in an addition or multiplication operation does not affect the result. For example:

   - Addition: $a + b = b + a$
   - Multiplication: $a \cdot b = b \cdot a$

   This property allows us to rearrange the terms in an expression without changing its value.

2. **Associative Property of Addition and Multiplication:** The associative property states that the grouping of terms in an addition or multiplication operation does not affect the result. For example:

   - Addition: $(a + b) + c = a + (b + c)$
   - Multiplication: $(a \cdot b) \cdot c = a \cdot (b \cdot c)$

   This property allows us to change the grouping of terms in an expression without altering its value.

3. **Distributive Property:** The distributive property, which we discussed in the previous section, allows us to distribute a value or term to each term inside parentheses. This property is particularly useful for simplifying expressions and performing operations more efficiently.

   For example, using the distributive property, we can simplify the expression $2 \cdot (x + y)$ as follows:

   $$2 \cdot (x + y) = 2 \cdot x + 2 \cdot y$$

   By distributing the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses, we obtain an equivalent expression.

These properties of equivalent algebraic expressions provide us with the flexibility to manipulate and simplify expressions while preserving their mathematical relationship. By applying these properties, we can transform complex expressions into simpler forms, making them easier to work with in algebraic calculations.

In the next section, we will explore various techniques for determining if two algebraic expressions are equivalent and how to simplify expressions using these techniques.

   b) $4y - 7$ and $-7 + 4y$

### Section: Equivalent Algebraic Expressions

Equivalent algebraic expressions possess certain properties that allow us to manipulate and simplify them. These properties are based on the fundamental rules of algebra and can be used to transform one expression into another while maintaining equivalence.

1. **Commutative Property of Addition and Multiplication:** The commutative property states that the order of terms in an addition or multiplication operation does not affect the result. For example:

   - Addition: $a + b = b + a$

### Subsection: Real World Applications

Understanding equivalent algebraic expressions is not only important for solving equations, but it also has real-world applications. Equivalent expressions can help us simplify and analyze real-life situations by representing them in a more concise and manageable form.

One common application of equivalent expressions is in financial calculations. For example, when calculating compound interest, we can use the formula $A = P(1 + r/n)^{nt}$, where $A$ represents the final amount, $P$ is the principal amount, $r$ is the annual interest rate, $n$ is the number of times interest is compounded per year, and $t$ is the number of years. By manipulating this formula using equivalent expressions, we can derive other useful formulas, such as the formula for calculating the principal amount or the annual interest rate.

Another real-world application of equivalent expressions is in physics. In physics, we often use mathematical equations to describe the relationships between different physical quantities. By manipulating these equations using equivalent expressions, we can derive new equations that provide insights into the behavior of physical systems. This allows us to make predictions and solve problems in various areas of physics, such as mechanics, electromagnetism, and thermodynamics.

It also has practical applications in various fields, including finance and physics. By mastering the properties of equivalent expressions, we can manipulate and analyze mathematical relationships more effectively, leading to a deeper understanding of the world around us.

### Section: Nested Fractions

In algebra, we often encounter complex expressions that involve fractions within fractions. These expressions are called nested fractions. Simplifying nested fractions is an important skill to master in order to solve algebraic equations efficiently.

#### Definition of Nested Fractions

A nested fraction is an expression that contains one or more fractions within another fraction. For example, consider the expression $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$. In this expression, the fraction $\frac{2}{3}$ is nested within the larger fraction $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$.

#### Simplifying Nested Fractions

To simplify nested fractions, we follow a step-by-step process:

1. Start by simplifying the innermost fraction. In the example above, we simplify $\frac{2}{3}$ to get $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$.

2. Next, we multiply the numerator and denominator of the outer fraction by the reciprocal of the inner fraction. In our example, we multiply $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$ by $\frac{3}{2}$ to get $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}} \cdot \frac{3}{2}$.

3. Multiply the numerators and denominators separately. In our example, we multiply $1$ and $3$ to get $3$, and $2$ and $1$ to get $2$. This gives us $\frac{3}{2}$.

4. Finally, simplify the resulting fraction, if possible. In our example, $\frac{3}{2}$ is already in its simplest form.

Let's look at another example to further illustrate the process. Consider the expression $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{\frac{3}{4}}}$. To simplify this nested fraction, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Simplify the innermost fraction $\frac{3}{4}$.

2. Multiply the numerator and denominator of the outer fraction by the reciprocal of the inner fraction $\frac{3}{4}$.

3. Multiply the numerators and denominators separately.

4. Simplify the resulting fraction, if possible.

By following these steps, we can simplify nested fractions and make algebraic expressions more manageable. Mastering this skill will greatly aid in solving complex equations involving nested fractions.

### Section: Nested Fractions

Nested fractions are a type of complex expression that involve fractions within fractions. They can appear daunting at first, but with practice and understanding of the simplification process, you can easily master them. In this section, we will explore the steps to simplify nested fractions and provide practice problems to reinforce your learning.

#### Definition of Nested Fractions

A nested fraction is an expression that contains one or more fractions within another fraction. For example, consider the expression $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$. In this expression, the fraction $\frac{2}{3}$ is nested within the larger fraction $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$.

#### Simplifying Nested Fractions

To simplify nested fractions, we follow a step-by-step process:

1. Start by simplifying the innermost fraction. In the example above, we simplify $\frac{2}{3}$ to get $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$.

2. Next, we multiply the numerator and denominator of the outer fraction by the reciprocal of the inner fraction. In our example, we multiply $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}$ by $\frac{3}{2}$ to get $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}} \cdot \frac{3}{2}$.

3. Multiply the numerators and denominators separately. In our example, we multiply $1$ and $3$ to get $3$, and $2$ and $1$ to get $2$. This gives us $\frac{3}{2}$.

4. Finally, simplify the resulting fraction, if possible. In our example, $\frac{3}{2}$ is already in its simplest form.

Let's look at another example to further illustrate the process. Consider the expression $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{\frac{3}{4}}}$. To simplify this nested fraction, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Simplify the innermost fraction $\frac{3}{4}$.

2. Multiply the numerator and denominator of the outer fraction by the reciprocal of the inner fraction $\frac{3}{4}$.

3. Multiply the numerators and denominators separately.

4. Simplify the resulting fraction, if possible.

By following these steps, we can simplify nested fractions and make algebraic expressions more manageable. Mastering this skill will greatly aid in solving complex equations involving nested fractions.

#### Practice Problems

Now, let's practice simplifying nested fractions with a few problems:

1. Simplify the expression $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{5}}$.

2. Simplify the expression $\frac{3}{\frac{4}{7}}$.

3. Simplify the expression $\frac{2}{\frac{3}{\frac{4}{9}}}$.

Take your time to solve these problems, and refer back to the steps we discussed earlier. Once you have found the simplified form of each expression, you can check your answers below.

1. $\frac{1}{\frac{2}{5}} = \frac{1}{\frac{2}{5}} \cdot \frac{5}{2} = \frac{1}{\frac{2}{5}} \cdot \frac{5}{2} = \frac{1 \cdot 5}{2 \cdot 2} = \frac{5}{4}$.

2. $\frac{3}{\frac{4}{7}} = \frac{3}{\frac{4}{7}} \cdot \frac{7}{4} = \frac{3}{\frac{4}{7}} \cdot \frac{7}{4} = \frac{3 \cdot 7}{4 \cdot 4} = \frac{21}{16}$.

3. $\frac{2}{\frac{3}{\frac{4}{9}}} = \frac{2}{\frac{3}{\frac{4}{9}}} \cdot \frac{9}{4} = \frac{2}{\frac{3}{\frac{4}{9}}} \cdot \frac{9}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot 9}{3 \cdot 4} = \frac{18}{12} = \frac{3}{2}$.

Congratulations! You have successfully simplified the nested fractions in the practice problems. Keep practicing to strengthen your understanding of nested fractions and their simplification process.

#### Real World Applications

Nested fractions may seem abstract, but they have practical applications in various fields. Let's explore a few real-world scenarios where nested fractions can be useful:

1. **Engineering**: Engineers often encounter complex calculations involving nested fractions when designing structures or analyzing data. For example, when calculating the stress on a bridge, engineers may need to simplify nested fractions to determine the load-bearing capacity.

Furthermore, we discussed the importance of understanding the properties of algebraic expressions, such as the commutative, associative, and distributive properties. These properties provide us with useful tools for manipulating expressions and simplifying complex equations.

By mastering the concepts covered in this chapter, you have developed a solid foundation in algebraic expressions. You are now equipped with the knowledge and skills necessary to tackle more advanced topics in algebra, such as equations, functions, and polynomials.

Simplify the following algebraic expression: $3x + 2y - 4x + 5y$

#### Exercise 2
Evaluate the expression $2x^2 - 3xy + 4y^2$ for $x = 2$ and $y = -1$.

#### Exercise 3
Solve the equation $2(x + 3) = 10$ for $x$.

#### Exercise 4
Simplify the expression $4(2x - 3y) - 2(3x + 4y)$.

#### Exercise 5
Solve the inequality $3x + 5 > 2x - 1$ for $x$.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of algebraic expressions. We began by understanding the basic components of an algebraic expression, such as variables, constants, and operators. We then learned how to combine these components to form expressions and how to simplify them using the order of operations.

We also delved into the concept of evaluating algebraic expressions, where we substituted specific values for the variables and calculated the result. This process allowed us to solve equations and inequalities, enabling us to find solutions to various mathematical problems.

Furthermore, we discussed the importance of understanding the properties of algebraic expressions, such as the commutative, associative, and distributive properties. These properties provide us with useful tools for manipulating expressions and simplifying complex equations.

By mastering the concepts covered in this chapter, you have developed a solid foundation in algebraic expressions. You are now equipped with the knowledge and skills necessary to tackle more advanced topics in algebra, such as equations, functions, and polynomials.

In the next chapter, we will build upon this foundation and explore the world of equations, where we will learn how to solve linear and quadratic equations, as well as systems of equations. Get ready to take your algebraic skills to the next level!

## Chapter: Linear Equations and Inequalities

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Linear Equations and Inequalities! In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts and techniques related to solving linear equations and inequalities. 

Linear equations and inequalities are essential tools in algebra that allow us to represent and solve real-world problems. They provide a way to describe relationships between variables and find solutions that satisfy certain conditions. Whether you are interested in solving equations or understanding the behavior of inequalities, this chapter will equip you with the necessary skills to master these concepts.

Throughout this chapter, we will cover various topics related to linear equations and inequalities. We will start by introducing the basic concepts of linear equations, such as variables, coefficients, and constants. We will then delve into different methods of solving linear equations, including the substitution method, elimination method, and graphing method. Additionally, we will explore the concept of slope-intercept form and how it can be used to graph linear equations.

Moving on, we will shift our focus to linear inequalities. We will learn how to solve and graph linear inequalities, as well as how to interpret their solutions in the context of real-world scenarios. We will also discuss systems of linear inequalities and how they can be solved graphically.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of linear equations and inequalities, enabling you to confidently solve a wide range of problems involving these concepts. So let's dive in and start mastering algebra with linear equations and inequalities!

### Section: One-step Equations

In this section, we will explore the concept of one-step equations. One-step equations are linear equations that can be solved in a single step. They involve only one operation, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

#### Definition and Solution

A one-step equation is an equation that can be solved by performing a single operation on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the value of the variable that makes the equation true.

To solve a one-step equation, follow these steps:

1. Identify the operation being performed on the variable. This could be addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.
2. Perform the inverse operation on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The inverse operation undoes the original operation.
3. Simplify both sides of the equation until you have the variable isolated on one side and the constant on the other side.
4. Check your solution by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation. The equation should be true if the solution is correct.

Let's look at an example to illustrate the process:

Example 1:
Solve the equation: $3x + 5 = 20$

1. The operation being performed on the variable is addition.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is subtraction. Subtract 5 from both sides of the equation:
   $3x + 5 - 5 = 20 - 5$
   Simplifying, we get: $3x = 15$
3. The variable is still being multiplied by 3. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 3:
   $\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{15}{3}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x = 5$
4. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
   $3(5) + 5 = 20$
   Simplifying, we get: $15 + 5 = 20$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

In this example, the solution to the one-step equation $3x + 5 = 20$ is $x = 5$. By following the steps outlined above, we were able to isolate the variable and find the value that satisfies the equation.

Now that we understand the concept of one-step equations and how to solve them, let's practice solving a few more examples to solidify our understanding.

### Section: One-step Equations

In this section, we will continue our exploration of one-step equations. One-step equations are linear equations that can be solved in a single step. They involve only one operation, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

#### Definition and Solution

A one-step equation is an equation that can be solved by performing a single operation on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the value of the variable that makes the equation true.

To solve a one-step equation, follow these steps:

1. Identify the operation being performed on the variable. This could be addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.
2. Perform the inverse operation on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The inverse operation undoes the original operation.
3. Simplify both sides of the equation until you have the variable isolated on one side and the constant on the other side.
4. Check your solution by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation. The equation should be true if the solution is correct.

Let's look at some more examples to illustrate the process:

#### Example 1:
Solve the equation: $2x - 3 = 9$

1. The operation being performed on the variable is subtraction.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is addition. Add 3 to both sides of the equation:
   $2x - 3 + 3 = 9 + 3$
   Simplifying, we get: $2x = 12$
3. The variable is still being multiplied by 2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 2:
   $\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{12}{2}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x = 6$
4. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
   $2(6) - 3 = 9$
   Simplifying, we get: $12 - 3 = 9$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

#### Example 2:
Solve the equation: $4y + 7 = 31$

1. The operation being performed on the variable is addition.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is subtraction. Subtract 7 from both sides of the equation:
   $4y + 7 - 7 = 31 - 7$
   Simplifying, we get: $4y = 24$
3. The variable is still being multiplied by 4. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 4:
   $\frac{4y}{4} = \frac{24}{4}$
   Simplifying, we get: $y = 6$
4. To check our solution, substitute the value of y back into the original equation:
   $4(6) + 7 = 31$
   Simplifying, we get: $24 + 7 = 31$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

By following these steps, you can solve one-step equations and find the values of the variables that make the equations true. Practice solving more one-step equations to strengthen your understanding of this concept.

### Section: One-step Equations

In this section, we will continue our exploration of one-step equations. One-step equations are linear equations that can be solved in a single step. They involve only one operation, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

#### Definition and Solution

A one-step equation is an equation that can be solved by performing a single operation on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the value of the variable that makes the equation true.

To solve a one-step equation, follow these steps:

1. Identify the operation being performed on the variable. This could be addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.
2. Perform the inverse operation on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The inverse operation undoes the original operation.
3. Simplify both sides of the equation until you have the variable isolated on one side and the constant on the other side.
4. Check your solution by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation. The equation should be true if the solution is correct.

Let's look at some more examples to illustrate the process:

#### Example 1:
Solve the equation: $2x - 3 = 9$

1. The operation being performed on the variable is subtraction.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is addition. Add 3 to both sides of the equation:
   $2x - 3 + 3 = 9 + 3$
   Simplifying, we get: $2x = 12$
3. The variable is still being multiplied by 2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 2:
   $\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{12}{2}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x = 6$
4. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
   $2(6) - 3 = 9$
   Simplifying, we get: $12 - 3 = 9$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

#### Example 2:
Solve the equation: $4y + 7 = 31$

1. The operation being performed on the variable is addition.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is subtraction. Subtract 7 from both sides of the equation:
   $4y + 7 - 7 = 31 - 7$
   Simplifying, we get: $4y = 24$
3. The variable is still being multiplied by 4. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 4:
   $\frac{4y}{4} = \frac{24}{4}$
   Simplifying, we get: $y = 6$
4. To check our solution, substitute the value of y back into the original equation:
   $4(6) + 7 = 31$
   Simplifying, we get: $24 + 7 = 31$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

#### Real World Applications

One-step equations have many real-world applications. They can be used to solve problems involving money, distance, time, and more. Let's look at a few examples:

##### Example 1:
Suppose you are shopping and you want to buy a pair of shoes that cost $50. You have already saved $20. How much more money do you need to save to buy the shoes?

Let's represent the amount of money you need to save as the variable $x$. The equation can be written as:

$20 + x = 50$

To solve for $x$, we need to isolate it on one side of the equation. Since $x$ is being added to 20, we can perform the inverse operation, which is subtraction. Subtract 20 from both sides of the equation:

$20 + x - 20 = 50 - 20$

Simplifying, we get:

$x = 30$

You need to save an additional $30 to buy the shoes.

##### Example 2:
Suppose you are driving to a friend's house, which is 150 miles away. You have already driven 80 miles. How many more miles do you need to drive to reach your friend's house?

Let's represent the number of miles you need to drive as the variable $x$. The equation can be written as:

$80 + x = 150$

To solve for $x$, we need to isolate it on one side of the equation. Since $x$ is being added to 80, we can perform the inverse operation, which is subtraction. Subtract 80 from both sides of the equation:

$80 + x - 80 = 150 - 80$

Simplifying, we get:

$x = 70$

You need to drive an additional 70 miles to reach your friend's house.

These examples demonstrate how one-step equations can be used to solve real-world problems. By understanding the concept of one-step equations and how to solve them, you will be better equipped to tackle various mathematical and practical challenges.

### Section: Two-step Equations

In this section, we will explore two-step equations, which are linear equations that require two operations to solve. These equations involve two different operations, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

#### Definition and Solution

A two-step equation is an equation that can be solved by performing two operations on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the value of the variable that makes the equation true.

To solve a two-step equation, follow these steps:

1. Identify the operations being performed on the variable. This could involve addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.
2. Perform the inverse operations on both sides of the equation to isolate the variable. The inverse operations undo the original operations.
3. Simplify both sides of the equation until you have the variable isolated on one side and the constant on the other side.
4. Check your solution by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation. The equation should be true if the solution is correct.

Let's look at an example to illustrate the process:

#### Example:
Solve the equation: $3x + 5 = 17$

1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 5 from both sides of the equation:
   $3x + 5 - 5 = 17 - 5$
   Simplifying, we get: $3x = 12$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 3, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 3:
   $\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{12}{3}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x = 4$
3. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
   $3(4) + 5 = 17$
   Simplifying, we get: $12 + 5 = 17$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

By following these steps, you can successfully solve two-step equations. Practice solving more examples to strengthen your understanding of this concept.

### Section: Two-step Equations

In this section, we will continue our exploration of two-step equations. We have already learned that two-step equations are linear equations that require two operations to solve. These equations involve two different operations, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

Now, let's dive deeper into solving two-step equations by practicing with some example problems.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the equation: $2x + 3 = 11$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 3 from both sides of the equation:
      $2x + 3 - 3 = 11 - 3$
      Simplifying, we get: $2x = 8$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 2:
      $\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{8}{2}$
      Simplifying, we get: $x = 4$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
      $2(4) + 3 = 11$
      Simplifying, we get: $8 + 3 = 11$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

2. Solve the equation: $5y - 2 = 13$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are subtraction and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, add 2 to both sides of the equation:
      $5y - 2 + 2 = 13 + 2$
      Simplifying, we get: $5y = 15$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 5, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 5:
      $\frac{5y}{5} = \frac{15}{5}$
      Simplifying, we get: $y = 3$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of y back into the original equation:
      $5(3) - 2 = 13$
      Simplifying, we get: $15 - 2 = 13$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

3. Solve the equation: $4z + 7 = 31$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 7 from both sides of the equation:
      $4z + 7 - 7 = 31 - 7$
      Simplifying, we get: $4z = 24$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 4, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 4:
      $\frac{4z}{4} = \frac{24}{4}$
      Simplifying, we get: $z = 6$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of z back into the original equation:
      $4(6) + 7 = 31$
      Simplifying, we get: $24 + 7 = 31$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

By practicing these example problems, you can gain confidence in solving two-step equations. Remember to follow the steps we discussed earlier: identify the operations, perform the inverse operations, simplify, and check your solution. Keep practicing to strengthen your skills in solving two-step equations.

### Section: Two-step Equations

In this section, we will continue our exploration of two-step equations. We have already learned that two-step equations are linear equations that require two operations to solve. These equations involve two different operations, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

Now, let's dive deeper into solving two-step equations by practicing with some example problems.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the equation: $2x + 3 = 11$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 3 from both sides of the equation:
      $2x + 3 - 3 = 11 - 3$
      Simplifying, we get: $2x = 8$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 2:
      $\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{8}{2}$
      Simplifying, we get: $x = 4$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
      $2(4) + 3 = 11$
      Simplifying, we get: $8 + 3 = 11$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

2. Solve the equation: $5y - 2 = 13$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are subtraction and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, add 2 to both sides of the equation:
      $5y - 2 + 2 = 13 + 2$
      Simplifying, we get: $5y = 15$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 5, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 5:
      $\frac{5y}{5} = \frac{15}{5}$
      Simplifying, we get: $y = 3$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of y back into the original equation:
      $5(3) - 2 = 13$
      Simplifying, we get: $15 - 2 = 13$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

3. Solve the equation: $4z + 7 = ?$

   In this problem, we are given an equation with the variable z. To solve for z, we need to isolate it on one side of the equation.
   
   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 7 from both sides of the equation:
      $4z + 7 - 7 = ? - 7$
      Simplifying, we get: $4z = ? - 7$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 4, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 4:
      $\frac{4z}{4} = \frac{? - 7}{4}$
      Simplifying, we get: $z = \frac{? - 7}{4}$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of z back into the original equation and solve for the missing value.

#### Subsection: Real World Applications

Two-step equations are not just abstract concepts; they have real-world applications as well. Let's explore some examples of how two-step equations can be used to solve real-world problems.

1. Example: Calculating the Cost of a Movie Ticket

   Suppose you want to go to the movies and the cost of a ticket is $12. However, you also need to pay a $3 service fee. Let's use a two-step equation to calculate the total cost of the movie ticket.

   Let's represent the total cost of the movie ticket as C. We can set up the equation as follows:
   $12 + 3 = C$

   To solve for C, we need to isolate it on one side of the equation. First, subtract 3 from both sides of the equation:
   $12 + 3 - 3 = C - 3$
   Simplifying, we get: $12 = C - 3$

   Next, to isolate C, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is addition. Add 3 to both sides of the equation:
   $12 + 3 = C - 3 + 3$
   Simplifying, we get: $15 = C$

   Therefore, the total cost of the movie ticket is $15.

2. Example: Determining the Number of Apples in a Basket

   Suppose you have a basket of apples and you know that there are 8 more than twice the number of apples in the basket. Let's use a two-step equation to determine the number of apples in the basket.

   Let's represent the number of apples in the basket as A. We can set up the equation as follows:
   $A = 2A + 8$

   To solve for A, we need to isolate it on one side of the equation. First, subtract 2A from both sides of the equation:
   $A - 2A = 2A + 8 - 2A$
   Simplifying, we get: $-A = 8$

   Next, to isolate A, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is multiplication. Multiply both sides of the equation by -1:
   $-1 \cdot -A = 8 \cdot -1$
   Simplifying, we get: $A = -8$

   Therefore, the number of apples in the basket is -8. However, since we cannot have a negative number of apples, this solution is not valid in this context.

   In real-world applications, it is important to consider the context and discard solutions that do not make sense.

These examples demonstrate how two-step equations can be used to solve real-world problems. By understanding the concepts and techniques of solving two-step equations, you will be able to apply them to various situations and improve your problem-solving skills.

### Section: Multi-step Equations

In this section, we will build upon our knowledge of solving equations by exploring multi-step equations. Multi-step equations are linear equations that require more than two operations to solve. These equations involve a combination of addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division.

#### Definition and Solution

A multi-step equation is an equation that requires multiple steps to isolate the variable and find its value. To solve a multi-step equation, we need to perform inverse operations to isolate the variable on one side of the equation.

Let's look at an example to understand the process of solving multi-step equations:

**Example: Solve the equation $3x + 5 = 17$**

Solution:
1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 5 from both sides of the equation:
   $3x + 5 - 5 = 17 - 5$
   Simplifying, we get: $3x = 12$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 3, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 3:
   $\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{12}{3}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x = 4$
3. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
   $3(4) + 5 = 17$
   Simplifying, we get: $12 + 5 = 17$
   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

In this example, we followed the steps of subtracting 5 and then dividing by 3 to isolate the variable and find its value. It is important to perform the inverse operations in the correct order to solve multi-step equations accurately.

Now, let's practice solving more multi-step equations to strengthen our understanding.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the equation: $2y + 4 = 10$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 4 from both sides of the equation:
      $2y + 4 - 4 = 10 - 4$
      Simplifying, we get: $2y = 6$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 2:
      $\frac{2y}{2} = \frac{6}{2}$
      Simplifying, we get: $y = 3$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of y back into the original equation:
      $2(3) + 4 = 10$
      Simplifying, we get: $6 + 4 = 10$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

2. Solve the equation: $4z - 3 = 9$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are subtraction and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, add 3 to both sides of the equation:
      $4z - 3 + 3 = 9 + 3$
      Simplifying, we get: $4z = 12$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 4, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 4:
      $\frac{4z}{4} = \frac{12}{4}$
      Simplifying, we get: $z = 3$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of z back into the original equation:
      $4(3) - 3 = 9$
      Simplifying, we get: $12 - 3 = 9$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

By following the steps of performing inverse operations, we can solve multi-step equations and find the values of the variables. Practice solving more multi-step equations to strengthen your skills in algebra.

   The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

In this example, we followed the steps of subtracting 5 and then dividing by 3 to isolate the variable and find its value. It is important to perform the inverse operations in the correct order to solve multi-step equations accurately.

Now, let's practice solving more multi-step equations to strengthen our understanding.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the equation: $2y + 4 = 10$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are addition and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, subtract 4 from both sides of the equation:
      $2y + 4 - 4 = 10 - 4$
      Simplifying, we get: $2y = 6$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 2:
      $\frac{2y}{2} = \frac{6}{2}$
      Simplifying, we get: $y = 3$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of y back into the original equation:
      $2(3) + 4 = 10$
      Simplifying, we get: $6 + 4 = 10$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

   Therefore, the solution to the equation $2y + 4 = 10$ is $y = 3$.

2. Solve the equation: $4x - 7 = 5$

   Solution:
   1. The operations being performed on the variable are subtraction and multiplication.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operations. First, add 7 to both sides of the equation:
      $4x - 7 + 7 = 5 + 7$
      Simplifying, we get: $4x = 12$
      Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 4, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the equation by 4:
      $\frac{4x}{4} = \frac{12}{4}$
      Simplifying, we get: $x = 3$
   3. To check our solution, substitute the value of x back into the original equation:
      $4(3) - 7 = 5$
      Simplifying, we get: $12 - 7 = 5$
      The equation is true, so our solution is correct.

   Therefore, the solution to the equation $4x - 7 = 5$ is $x = 3$.

By practicing more multi-step equations, we can become more proficient in solving them. Remember to carefully perform the inverse operations in the correct order to isolate the variable and find its value.

### Section: One-step Inequalities

In this section, we will explore one-step inequalities. An inequality is a mathematical statement that compares two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). One-step inequalities involve only one operation to solve.

#### Definition and Solution

A one-step inequality is an inequality that can be solved by performing a single operation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

Let's look at an example to understand the process of solving one-step inequalities:

**Example: Solve the inequality $2x + 3 > 7$**

Solution:
1. The operation being performed on the variable is addition.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, subtract 3 from both sides of the inequality:
   $2x + 3 - 3 > 7 - 3$
   Simplifying, we get: $2x > 4$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 2:
   $\frac{2x}{2} > \frac{4}{2}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x > 2$
3. The solution to the inequality is $x > 2$. This means that any value of x greater than 2 will satisfy the inequality.

In this example, we subtracted 3 from both sides of the inequality and then divided by 2 to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality. It is important to perform the inverse operation in the correct order to solve one-step inequalities accurately.

Now, let's practice solving more one-step inequalities to strengthen our understanding.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the inequality: $3y - 5 < 10$

   Solution:
   1. The operation being performed on the variable is subtraction.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, add 5 to both sides of the inequality:
   $3y - 5 + 5 < 10 + 5$
   Simplifying, we get: $3y < 15$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 3, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 3:
   $\frac{3y}{3} < \frac{15}{3}$
   Simplifying, we get: $y < 5$
   3. The solution to the inequality is $y < 5$. This means that any value of y less than 5 will satisfy the inequality.

In this section, we learned about one-step inequalities and how to solve them by performing a single operation to isolate the variable. Remember to carefully follow the order of operations and perform the inverse operation correctly. Practice solving more one-step inequalities to strengthen your skills in this topic.

### Section: One-step Inequalities

In this section, we will continue our exploration of one-step inequalities. As a quick recap, an inequality is a mathematical statement that compares two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). One-step inequalities involve only one operation to solve.

#### Definition and Solution

A one-step inequality is an inequality that can be solved by performing a single operation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

Let's look at another example to further understand the process of solving one-step inequalities:

**Example: Solve the inequality $3x - 2 \geq 7$**

Solution:
1. The operation being performed on the variable is subtraction.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, add 2 to both sides of the inequality:
   $3x - 2 + 2 \geq 7 + 2$
   Simplifying, we get: $3x \geq 9$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 3, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 3:
   $\frac{3x}{3} \geq \frac{9}{3}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x \geq 3$
3. The solution to the inequality is $x \geq 3$. This means that any value of x greater than or equal to 3 will satisfy the inequality.

In this example, we added 2 to both sides of the inequality and then divided by 3 to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality. It is important to perform the inverse operation in the correct order to solve one-step inequalities accurately.

Now, let's practice solving more one-step inequalities to strengthen our understanding.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the inequality: $2y + 4 < 12$

   Solution:
   1. The operation being performed on the variable is addition.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, subtract 4 from both sides of the inequality:
   $2y + 4 - 4 < 12 - 4$
   Simplifying, we get: $2y < 8$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 2:
   $\frac{2y}{2} < \frac{8}{2}$
   Simplifying, we get: $y < 4$
   3. The solution to the inequality is $y < 4$. This means that any value of y less than 4 will satisfy the inequality.

2. Solve the inequality: $5z - 3 \leq 22$

   Solution:
   1. The operation being performed on the variable is subtraction.
   2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, add 3 to both sides of the inequality:
   $5z - 3 + 3 \leq 22 + 3$
   Simplifying, we get: $5z \leq 25$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 5, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 5:
   $\frac{5z}{5} \leq \frac{25}{5}$
   Simplifying, we get: $z \leq 5$
   3. The solution to the inequality is $z \leq 5$. This means that any value of z less than or equal to 5 will satisfy the inequality.

These practice problems should help reinforce your understanding of solving one-step inequalities. Remember to carefully identify the operation being performed on the variable and perform the inverse operation to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

### Section: One-step Inequalities

In this section, we will continue our exploration of one-step inequalities. As a quick recap, an inequality is a mathematical statement that compares two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). One-step inequalities involve only one operation to solve.

#### Definition and Solution

A one-step inequality is an inequality that can be solved by performing a single operation to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

Let's look at another example to further understand the process of solving one-step inequalities:

**Example: Solve the inequality $3x - 2 \geq 7$**

Solution:
1. The operation being performed on the variable is subtraction.
2. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, add 2 to both sides of the inequality:
   $3x - 2 + 2 \geq 7 + 2$
   Simplifying, we get: $3x \geq 9$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 3, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 3:
   $\frac{3x}{3} \geq \frac{9}{3}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x \geq 3$
3. The solution to the inequality is $x \geq 3$. This means that any value of x greater than or equal to 3 will satisfy the inequality.

In this example, we added 2 to both sides of the inequality and then divided by 3 to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality. It is important to perform the inverse operation in the correct order to solve one-step inequalities accurately.

Now, let's explore some real-world applications of one-step inequalities.

#### Subsection: Real World Applications

One-step inequalities can be used to solve various real-world problems. They help us determine the range of values that satisfy certain conditions or constraints. Let's look at a few examples:

**Example 1:**

Suppose you are planning a road trip and want to calculate the maximum distance you can travel within a given time frame. Let's say your car can travel at a constant speed of 60 miles per hour. You have a total of 8 hours available for the trip. To find the maximum distance you can travel, you can use the following inequality:

$60 \times t \leq d$

Where:
- $t$ represents the time in hours
- $d$ represents the distance in miles

By substituting the given values, we can solve the inequality to find the maximum distance:

$60 \times 8 \leq d$

Simplifying, we get:

$480 \leq d$

Therefore, the maximum distance you can travel within 8 hours is 480 miles.

**Example 2:**

Let's consider a scenario where you are buying concert tickets. The ticket price is $50 per ticket, and you have a budget of $200. To determine the maximum number of tickets you can buy, you can use the following inequality:

$50 \times n \leq 200$

Where:
- $n$ represents the number of tickets

By solving the inequality, we can find the maximum number of tickets:

$50 \times n \leq 200$

Dividing both sides by 50, we get:

$n \leq 4$

Therefore, you can buy a maximum of 4 tickets within your budget of $200.

These examples demonstrate how one-step inequalities can be used to solve real-world problems and make informed decisions based on given constraints. By understanding and applying the principles of one-step inequalities, you can solve a wide range of practical problems.

### Section: Two-step Inequalities

In this section, we will delve into the topic of two-step inequalities. Similar to one-step inequalities, two-step inequalities are mathematical statements that compare two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). However, unlike one-step inequalities, two-step inequalities require two operations to solve.

#### Definition and Solution

A two-step inequality is an inequality that can be solved by performing two operations to isolate the variable. The goal is to find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

Let's look at an example to further understand the process of solving two-step inequalities:

**Example: Solve the inequality $2x + 5 \leq 13$**

Solution:
1. The first operation being performed on the variable is multiplication. To isolate the variable, we need to perform the inverse operation. First, subtract 5 from both sides of the inequality:
   $2x + 5 - 5 \leq 13 - 5$
   Simplifying, we get: $2x \leq 8$
   Next, since the variable is being multiplied by 2, we need to perform the inverse operation, which is division. Divide both sides of the inequality by 2:
   $\frac{2x}{2} \leq \frac{8}{2}$
   Simplifying, we get: $x \leq 4$
2. The solution to the inequality is $x \leq 4$. This means that any value of x less than or equal to 4 will satisfy the inequality.

In this example, we subtracted 5 from both sides of the inequality and then divided by 2 to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality. It is important to perform the inverse operations in the correct order to solve two-step inequalities accurately.

Now, let's explore some real-world applications of two-step inequalities.

### Section: Two-step Inequalities

In this section, we will continue our exploration of two-step inequalities. We have already learned that two-step inequalities are mathematical statements that compare two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). We have also learned that solving two-step inequalities involves performing two operations to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

Now, let's dive deeper into the process of solving two-step inequalities by looking at some practice problems.

#### Practice Problems

1. Solve the inequality $3x - 7 > 14$

2. Solve the inequality $2(4 - x) \geq 10$

3. Solve the inequality $\frac{1}{2}x + 3 \leq 5$

4. Solve the inequality $-2(2x + 1) < -10$

5. Solve the inequality $4x + 8 \geq 20$

Take your time to solve each problem step by step. Remember to perform the inverse operations in the correct order to isolate the variable and find the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

Once you have solved the practice problems, you can check your answers below:

#### Practice Problems - Answers

1. $x > 7$

2. $x \leq -1$

3. $x \leq 4$

4. $x > -3$

5. $x \geq 3$

Congratulations on completing the practice problems! Solving two-step inequalities can be challenging, but with practice, you will become more comfortable with the process.
